"Glen,\n\nInteresting reading from one of our customers. Kim.\n\n-----Original Message-----\nFrom: Gold, Amy [mailto:AGold@coral-energy.com]\nSent: Wednesday, August 01, 2001 11:25 AM\nTo: Watson, Kimberly\nSubject: FW: New Report on Lessons from California\n\n\nI just got this and thought you might find it interesting as well.\n\nAmy\n\n\n\n--------- Inline attachment follows ---------\n\nFrom:  <Eric@EHirst.com>\nTo: Eric@EHirst.com\nDate: Wednesday, August 1, 2001 12:16:55 GMT\nSubject: \n\nDear Colleague,\n\nThe Edison Electric Institute just published a paper, The California\nElectricity Crisis: Lessons for Other States, which I wrote. The paper is\navailable on the Publications page of my website, www.EHirst.com\n<http://www.ehirst.com/> . Please let me know if you have any comments on\nthe paper.\n\nHere is a summary of the paper:\n\n        California was supposed to show the rest of the nation a brighter\nelectricity future. That future, as outlined by the California Public\nUtilities Commission in 1994, envisioned an electricity industry featuring\ncompetitive markets. Such markets were intended to produce lower costs and\nmore choices for consumers, while ensuring profits for efficient energy\nsuppliers and encouraging the retirement of old, inefficient power plants.\n\n        Although the first two years of restructuring (beginning in April\n1998) seemed to bear out these promises, the last year has been a disaster\nfor California's electricity consumers, taxpayers, and electric utilities.\nPart of the fallout from California's electricity crisis is considerable\nconcern-even opposition-in other states to restructuring the electricity\nindustry. \n\n        Although a few states have decided, based on the California\nexperience, not to restructure, I believe that is the wrong outcome.\nInstead, the primary lessons other states should learn from California are\nthat competitive markets can work; competition is not to blame for\nCalifornia's problems; and basic economic principles of supply, demand, and\nprices affect market outcomes.\n\n        Section 2 of this paper briefly reviews the history of electricity\nregulation and restructuring in California and the problems that occurred\nbeginning in May 2000. Section 3, the heart of this paper, offers guidance\nto other states on the key ingredients of a competitive electricity\nindustry. This section makes several points: \n\n*\tMaintain a favorable investment and regulatory climate for new\ngeneration to ensure that enough generation capacity is online and planned\nto meet growing loads. \n\n*\tEnsure that enough infrastructure (transmission capacity and natural\ngas pipeline capacity) is in place and planned to meet growing loads,\nmaintain reliability, connect new generators to the grid, support large\nregional markets, and provide fuel to power plants. \n\n*\tEncourage retail customers to participate in dynamic-pricing and\nvoluntary-load-reduction programs (i.e., couple retail and wholesale\nmarkets). \n\n*\tCreate honest retail competition: avoid standard-offer rates with\nartificial discounts and create conditions that encourage many companies to\noffer retail services without favoring individual competitors. \n\n*\tEncourage electricity suppliers to manage and diversify their supply\nand price risks. \n\n*\tCreate efficient and integrated wholesale markets for energy,\nancillary services, and transmission congestion. \n\n*\tMonitor and minimize horizontal market power (generation) and\nvertical market power (combined ownership and operation of generation and\ntransmission). \n\n\n        Ultimately, competitive markets for electricity will lower costs and\nprices, better align consumer prices with producer costs, improve producer\nefficiency in both investment and operation, maintain or improve\nreliability, and yield greater innovation in customer services and pricing\noptions. \n\n        But, this transition from one industry structure to another is\nturning out to be long and complicated. It is complicated because\nelectricity is so important to our modern, high-tech society; electricity is\nour most flexible fuel, providing light, heat, motive power, and the energy\nto operate all our electronic equipment. Also, electricity truly is a\nreal-time product for which production and consumption must occur at the\nsame time. \n\n        Although the problems that have occurred in California are\nsubstantial and worrisome, regulators and legislators in other states should\nfocus on the long-term benefits of competitive electricity markets and\naccept the possible short-term problems that may occur. They also need to\nrecognize that wholesale markets are regional in scope and extend far beyond\nthe boundaries of any single state. Finally, competition in retail and\nwholesale markets must be coordinated; in particular, it is not possible to\nhave competitive wholesale markets unless at least some retail load faces\ntime varying prices. \n\n        If states restructure properly to create competitive markets for\nwholesale energy and retail services, we should all benefit. Consumers will\nenjoy lower prices, they will have many more choices of energy services and\nprice-risk tradeoffs, reliability will be improved because most reliability\nservices will be obtained through markets instead of by engineering edict,\nand the financial risks associated with building and operating power plants\nwill be assigned properly to investors rather than consumers. \n\nEric\n----------------------------------------------\nEric Hirst \nConsulting in Electric-Industry Restructuring\n106 Capital Circle\nOak Ridge, TN 37830\n*865-482-5470 (phone & fax)    *Eric@EHirst.com\nhttp://www.EHirst.com <http://www.ehirst.com/> "

"Glen,\n\nPlease add Paul Y'Barbo and Mark McConnell to your distribution list for marketing.  \n\nThanks, Kim.\n\n\n-----Original Message-----\nFrom:\tHass, Glen\nSent:\tMon 10/1/2001 4:45 PM\nTo:\tMiller, Mary Kay; Kilmer III, Robert; Brennan, Lorna; Donoho, Lindy; Harris, Steven; Lindberg, Lorraine; Lohman, TK; Lokay, Michelle; Moore, Jan; Watson, Kimberly\nCc:\t\nSubject:\tCEC Infrastructure Report\n\nThe California Energy Commission has issued its final report on the California Infrastructure which is to be voted upon by the Commision on Wednesday.  Below is a summary of the report that was in Inside Ferc and attached is a copy of the full report (123 pages).  There's an executive summary at the beginning which is about 10 pages.  Although, as stated in the summary, the Commission found a lack of sufficient capacity on El Paso as a contributor to high prices, they do not take on the issue of market power as had been suggested by the CPUC and other commenters and blame the pipeline for such prices.  Instead they cite El Paso capacity problems as one of several factors which caused high prices last winter.  I'll keep you advised as to what happens on Wedneday at the CEC meeting.  gh\n\n\n\nIN NEW REPORT, CEC COMMITTEE KEEPS EMPHASIS ON IN-STATE GAS CAPACITY\nConstrained interstate gas pipeline capacity was just one of the factors that led to high natural gas\nprices in California last fall and winter, according to a report issued last week by a committee of the\nCalifornia Energy Commission. The revised final report on gas infrastructure issues notably skirted the\nissue of market power as a factor in the gas price spikes that rocked the state.\nAfter taking into consideration comments from the California Public Utilities Commission and others,\nthe report to the full CEC concluded that a number of factors were involved. ?Insufficient receipt capacity\nwithin California and insufficient capacity on the El Paso [Natural Gas Co.] pipeline system both contrib-uted\nto the high price of natural gas in the fall and winter of 2000,? the Sept. 24 report said. The CEC is\nscheduled to vote Wednesday on the report.\nThe revised version took into account scarcity on the El Paso system but did not place the blame\nentirely on that, noting the high demand for gas-fired electric generation and relatively low storage levels\nduring times of high gas prices. ?As a result of the insufficient capacity both to and within California,\nbuyers were less able to force suppliers to bid against each other. Whether prices during this period were, in\nwhole or in part, the result of a free market?s rationing of scarce suppliers through a scarcity premium, or\nentirely the result of price manipulation, is beyond the scope of this report,? wrote Commissioner Michal\nMoore, presiding member of CEC?s electricity and natural gas committee.\nPointing to the high-profile CPUC complaint (RP00-241) against El Paso Corp. pending at FERC, the\nCEC added that it had ?every confidence that the interests of California ratepayers will be well-represented\nby the CPUC.?\nThe report pointed to the fact that California shippers surrendered large blocks of capacity on the El\nPaso system in recent years. ?The shortfall in capacity was absorbed by California, and not the upstream\ncustomers. This is due, in part, to the decision a few years ago by California to turn back part of its rights to\nfirm capacity on the El Paso system. It is also due to the failure by El Paso to honor its contractual commit-ment\nto deliver capacity paid for by California,? the report noted.\nMoore?s committee had postponed issuance of the report after receiving comments on a draft version\nfrom the CPUC, The Utility Reform Network and Southern California Edison Co., which all called for\nconsideration of allegations that El Paso exerted market power over gas sales into California (IF, 27 Aug,\n1). The draft pointed to insufficient takeaway capacity in California and called for slack capacity in the\nrange of 15% to 20% (IF, 13 Aug, 4).\nThe report went on to suggest that California ?can no longer plan for the future as if we lived in a\nregulated, cost-plus environment. Under price regulation, adverse hydroelectric conditions and extreme\ntemperatures did not lead to extreme price spikes. Under today?s conditions, that is no longer the case.?\nMoore added that the state?s ?virtual complete reliance? on gas for electric generation has contributed to\nthe new dynamics surrounding capacity planning.\nMore than 100,000 Mw of gas-fired generation proposed for the Western Systems Coordinating Council\nregion demands expansion of the regional pipeline grid, the report continued, saying the CEC was ?encour-aged?\nby recently announced expansion plans by interstate pipelines and FERC?s stated intent to expedite\nreview of such proposals. The report also noted that Southern California Gas Co. is developing 375,000 Mcf/\nday of additional receipt capacity, which should be ready by the end of the year. Also, Pacific Gas and Electric\nCo. plans to add as much as 600,000 Mcf/day of capacity by 2003, according to the report.\nIt observed that intrastate pipelines, in particular those in Southern California, ran at close to full\ncapacity at times this and last year, which created bottlenecks in delivering supplies from interstate\npipelines to gas consumers, including electric generators in 2000 and early 2001.\nHigh prices in the future could be held at bay with the in-state expansions and the CPUC?s recent approval\nof SoCal Gas? plan to remove 37 Bcf of cushion and working gas from three storage fields in Montebello and\nAliso Canyon in Los Angeles County and La Goleta in Santa Barbara County (IF, 2 July, 3).\nIn addition to strongly emphasizing intrastate needs, the report recommended that the state ?analyze\nthe consequences of the increasing upstream gas demands on the El Paso pipeline system as they affect the\nflows of natural gas and electricity? between California and the Southwest U.S. and Mexico.\nAccording to the report, the state also should: encourage in-state gas production; re-evaluate design criteria\nfor the intrastate system in consideration of growing power generation demand; consider gas curtailment\nmechanisms; ?optimize? storage; and encourage the creation of new storage facilities in the state.\nThe 123-page report is available on the CEC?s Web site (www.energy.ca.gov/reports/index.html).\n\n\nThe attachment below is the final CEC Infrastructure report:\n\n <<FinalCECInfraRpt.pdf>> "

"\nFYI, Kim.\n\n\n-----Original Message-----\nFrom: Gold, Amy [mailto:AGold@coral-energy.com]\nSent: Wednesday, August 01, 2001 11:25 AM\nTo: Watson, Kimberly\nSubject: FW: New Report on Lessons from California\n\n\nI just got this and thought you might find it interesting as well.\n\nAmy\n\n\n\n--------- Inline attachment follows ---------\n\nFrom:  <Eric@EHirst.com>\nTo: Eric@EHirst.com\nDate: Wednesday, August 1, 2001 12:16:55 GMT\nSubject: \n\nDear Colleague,\n\nThe Edison Electric Institute just published a paper, The California\nElectricity Crisis: Lessons for Other States, which I wrote. The paper is\navailable on the Publications page of my website, www.EHirst.com\n<http://www.ehirst.com/> . Please let me know if you have any comments on\nthe paper.\n\nHere is a summary of the paper:\n\n        California was supposed to show the rest of the nation a brighter\nelectricity future. That future, as outlined by the California Public\nUtilities Commission in 1994, envisioned an electricity industry featuring\ncompetitive markets. Such markets were intended to produce lower costs and\nmore choices for consumers, while ensuring profits for efficient energy\nsuppliers and encouraging the retirement of old, inefficient power plants.\n\n        Although the first two years of restructuring (beginning in April\n1998) seemed to bear out these promises, the last year has been a disaster\nfor California's electricity consumers, taxpayers, and electric utilities.\nPart of the fallout from California's electricity crisis is considerable\nconcern-even opposition-in other states to restructuring the electricity\nindustry. \n\n        Although a few states have decided, based on the California\nexperience, not to restructure, I believe that is the wrong outcome.\nInstead, the primary lessons other states should learn from California are\nthat competitive markets can work; competition is not to blame for\nCalifornia's problems; and basic economic principles of supply, demand, and\nprices affect market outcomes.\n\n        Section 2 of this paper briefly reviews the history of electricity\nregulation and restructuring in California and the problems that occurred\nbeginning in May 2000. Section 3, the heart of this paper, offers guidance\nto other states on the key ingredients of a competitive electricity\nindustry. This section makes several points: \n\n*\tMaintain a favorable investment and regulatory climate for new\ngeneration to ensure that enough generation capacity is online and planned\nto meet growing loads. \n\n*\tEnsure that enough infrastructure (transmission capacity and natural\ngas pipeline capacity) is in place and planned to meet growing loads,\nmaintain reliability, connect new generators to the grid, support large\nregional markets, and provide fuel to power plants. \n\n*\tEncourage retail customers to participate in dynamic-pricing and\nvoluntary-load-reduction programs (i.e., couple retail and wholesale\nmarkets). \n\n*\tCreate honest retail competition: avoid standard-offer rates with\nartificial discounts and create conditions that encourage many companies to\noffer retail services without favoring individual competitors. \n\n*\tEncourage electricity suppliers to manage and diversify their supply\nand price risks. \n\n*\tCreate efficient and integrated wholesale markets for energy,\nancillary services, and transmission congestion. \n\n*\tMonitor and minimize horizontal market power (generation) and\nvertical market power (combined ownership and operation of generation and\ntransmission). \n\n\n        Ultimately, competitive markets for electricity will lower costs and\nprices, better align consumer prices with producer costs, improve producer\nefficiency in both investment and operation, maintain or improve\nreliability, and yield greater innovation in customer services and pricing\noptions. \n\n        But, this transition from one industry structure to another is\nturning out to be long and complicated. It is complicated because\nelectricity is so important to our modern, high-tech society; electricity is\nour most flexible fuel, providing light, heat, motive power, and the energy\nto operate all our electronic equipment. Also, electricity truly is a\nreal-time product for which production and consumption must occur at the\nsame time. \n\n        Although the problems that have occurred in California are\nsubstantial and worrisome, regulators and legislators in other states should\nfocus on the long-term benefits of competitive electricity markets and\naccept the possible short-term problems that may occur. They also need to\nrecognize that wholesale markets are regional in scope and extend far beyond\nthe boundaries of any single state. Finally, competition in retail and\nwholesale markets must be coordinated; in particular, it is not possible to\nhave competitive wholesale markets unless at least some retail load faces\ntime varying prices. \n\n        If states restructure properly to create competitive markets for\nwholesale energy and retail services, we should all benefit. Consumers will\nenjoy lower prices, they will have many more choices of energy services and\nprice-risk tradeoffs, reliability will be improved because most reliability\nservices will be obtained through markets instead of by engineering edict,\nand the financial risks associated with building and operating power plants\nwill be assigned properly to investors rather than consumers. \n\nEric\n----------------------------------------------\nEric Hirst \nConsulting in Electric-Industry Restructuring\n106 Capital Circle\nOak Ridge, TN 37830\n*865-482-5470 (phone & fax)    *Eric@EHirst.com\nhttp://www.EHirst.com <http://www.ehirst.com/> "

"\n\n -----Original Message-----\nFrom: \tEnron Announcements/Corp/Enron@ENRON   On Behalf Of Ken Lay - Office of the Chairman@ENRON\nSent:\tFriday, November 30, 2001 5:58 PM\nTo:\tAll Enron Employees United States Group@ENRON\nSubject:\tUPDATE - Reporting to Work Next Week\n\n\nMany of you have asked whether you should come into the office next week, especially if there is no guarantee of being paid for time worked after today.  We want to encourage you to come to work.  We continue to consider every option available to us as a company; and unfortunately, restructuring through bankruptcy is one of those options.  Your continued efforts during these trying times are critical to the company as we go through this decision making process.\n\nI still cannot say with certainty if or when we will file for bankruptcy.  But you should be aware that, in general, the bankruptcy code provides protection for employee salaries and wages earned within 90 days of a petition filing to the extent of $4,650 per employee.  I want you to know that if we file a bankruptcy petition, we intend to seek approval by the bankruptcy court to exercise that protection immediately and pay you for your time worked prior to any petition filing according to our regular payroll practices.  Additionally, Enron would be obligated and will pay salaries and wages earned after a bankruptcy filing. For Enron, where our value is directly tied to our employees, making sure that we meet these obligations will be a high priority.\n\nWe are continuing to evaluate the ongoing resource needs of the company for each potential option we might pursue.  Of course, until we finalize those business decisions, we cannot make that determination.  I know that this prolonged process is agonizing for you.  I can only continue to assure you that we will notify you of your employment status as soon as we are able to do so.\n\nWhile terminated employees will tap into their own network of contacts as they look for new employment, we hope to do what we can to assist them.  We will tap into the contacts we have as a company to aid terminated employees in their job search.  We have already been in touch with several employers and many are interested in Enron alumni.  We will share more information on these efforts soon.\n\nI appreciate your continued patience and cooperation.\n\nKen"
